    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="bc1fb92c401b4546880e4b7eec09605b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'e6a80f4e-2f7c-4978-a7e2-1e2faea533aa'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d3386546" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Due to its high theoretical capacity, cobalt oxide (Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) has attracted attention to sodium-ion battery (SIB) anodes. However, its low conductivity and poor rate performance have limited its practical application. This work proposes a co-precipitation doping strategy to synthesize iron-doped Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles (Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs). Both experimental and theoretical results confirm that iron (Fe) doping at octahedral sites within spinel structures is a critical factor in enhancing rate performance. The decreased bandgap and enlarged ion transport spacing originate in Fe doping. This effectively facilitates the electron and Na-ion (Na<sup>+</sup>) transport during discharge/charge processes, delivering an impressive rate capability of 402.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−</sup>¹ at 3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−</sup>¹. The Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs demonstrate remarkable cycling stability. They maintain a high specific capacity of 786.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−</sup>¹ even after 500 cycles at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−</sup>¹, with no noticeable capacity fading. When assembled into a Na-ion full cell, a remarkable discharge capacity of 105&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with stable cycling performance is attained. This work provides valuable insights into the functional design of high-rate electrodes, offering a promising approach to addressing the critical challenges faced by sodium anodes.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202412449-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202412449-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The ever-increasing global energy demand has intensified the search for sustainable and efficient energy storage systems. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have gained significant attention among promising candidates due to their abundant resources, safety, and environmental friendliness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0001_R_d3386538e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Eng. J.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, SIBs suffer from low capacity and poor rate performance due to the large ionic radius of Na-ion (Na<sup>+</sup>) in practical applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0002_R_d3386538e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>-<a href="#smll202412449-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0004_R_d3386538e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Various materials have been developed as anodes to enhance the electrochemical performance of SIBs during charge and discharge processes, including carbon-based materials, transition metal sulfides (TMSs), alloying materials, and transition metal oxides (TMOs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0001_R_d3386538e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Eng. J.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Carbon-based materials have limited capacities due to their inadequate Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0005_R_d3386538e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nano Mater. Sci.">5</a>-<a href="#smll202412449-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0008_R_d3386538e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Rec.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Transition metal sulfides (TMSs) and alloying materials have been widely explored as anode candidates for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their high theoretical capacities and favorable reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0009_R_d3386538e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a>-<a href="#smll202412449-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0011_R_d3386538e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, TMSs often suffer from poor cycling stability caused by severe volume expansion and structural degradation during sodiation/desodiation. Additionally, their inherent low electrical conductivity hinders rate performance. On the other hand, alloying materials (such as Sn, Sb, and Bi-based compounds) exhibit high capacities through alloying-dealloying reactions but experience substantial volume changes, leading to rapid capacity fading and poor long-term durability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0012_R_d3386538e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Mater.">12</a>-<a href="#smll202412449-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0014_R_d3386538e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, transition metal oxides (TMOs) offer a more balanced electrochemical performance, featuring moderate capacity, improved structural stability, and enhanced cycling lifespan.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0001_R_d3386538e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Eng. J.">1</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0015_R_d3386538e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Phys. Chem. C">15</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0016_R_d3386538e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Appl. Sci.-Basel">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Their relatively stable crystal structures mitigate severe volume expansion, and their diverse redox chemistry contributes to better electrochemical reversibility. Therefore, TMOs emerge as promising alternatives with superior overall performance compared to TMSs and alloying materials for high-performance SIB anodes.</p>
         
         <p>Cobalt oxide (Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) has emerged as a potential anode material for SIBs due to its high theoretical capacity and behavior as a p-type semiconductor with favorable electronic properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0017_R_d3386538e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Alloys Compd.">17</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0018_R_d3386538e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intrinsic low electrical conductivity of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> has been a critical factor limiting its rate capability as an anode material. Furthermore, the sodium-ion diffusion within the bulk structure is constrained by a singular diffusion path, which hinders efficient Na⁺ transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0001_R_d3386538e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Eng. J.">1</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0004_R_d3386538e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small">4</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0019_R_d3386538e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode suffers from poor rate performance, with the impact of ion diffusion playing a more critical role than the associated volume expansion. Therefore, developing high-performance anode materials remains a critical challenge, particularly in achieving high-rate performance, which is essential for fast charging and high-power applications. Improving the anode material's ionic and electronic conductivity is the key to enhancing rate performance.</p>
         
         <p>Constructing porous structures is an innovative way to develop high-rate performance anode materials to improve sodium storage performance in metal-oxides-based anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0020_R_d3386538e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Alloys Compd.">20</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0021_R_d3386538e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Sci.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the porous structure alone does not effectively address the inherently low conductivity of metal oxides or sufficiently mitigate the constrained diffusion pathways. These limitations can lead to the accumulation of inactive Na<sup>+</sup> within the internal active material during repeated Na⁺ insertion and extraction cycles, resulting in “dead sodium”.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0022_R_d3386538e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another effective method is constructing rich intrinsic conductivity cobalt oxide with iron lattice doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0018_R_d3386538e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0023_R_d3386538e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Catal.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The improvement in electrical conductivity reduces impedance in the electron transport pathway, facilitating a more uniform and rapid electrochemical reaction. Notably, uniform reaction kinetics help mitigate the destructive effects of volume expansion, thereby reducing particle pulverization and material detachment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0010_R_d3386538e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Rare Met.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, varying lattice components can modulate the lengthening of metal-O bonds and the expansion of ion transport channels, leading to enhanced ionic kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0024_R_d3386538e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0025_R_d3386538e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Although constructing material with iron lattice doping has been shown to improve sodium storage properties, the anode capacity at the high-rate performance of the formed anodes seems unsatisfactory.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0026_R_d3386538e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">26</a>-<a href="#smll202412449-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0028_R_d3386538e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the functional mechanism of iron lattice doping on metal oxide is still being determined. Therefore, to understand electrochemical reactions at the atomic level, it is necessary to study the effect of iron lattice doping on sodium storage properties using a straightforward method.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we demonstrate an urchin-like hollow-structured electrode of iron lattice doping cobalt oxide nanoparticles (Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs) for SIBs, delivering a reversible capacity of 402.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and maintaining a high specific capacity of 786.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−</sup>¹ even after 500 cycles at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−</sup>¹, which makes this work the best performance of capacity/cycles ever reported so far. Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs show the unique high-rate performance of sodium ion storage. Electrochemical investigations and density functional theory (DFT) calculations, which have not been reported previously in cobalt oxide anodes, elucidated the mechanism and importance of iron lattice doping, which can improve the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient and increase the intrinsic conductivity. This work presents a general approach for preparing high-rate capacity electrode materials and also clearly explains the functional design of high-rate electrodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202412449-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202412449-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202412449-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202412449-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structure and Morphology</h3>
            
            <p>The urchin-like Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs were fabricated by the co-precipitation doping synthesis method. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202412449-fig-0001">1</a> shows the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images of the Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs sample prepared by the hydrothermal reaction of Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O, Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, Ammonium fluoride (NH<sub>4</sub>F) and Urea in deionized (DI) water. The urchin-like Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs sample with iron is a lot of hollow microspheres with diameters of 1–2&nbsp;µm and high surfaces (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0001">1c</a>). The surface nanorods with size of 20–80&nbsp;nm were grown, and subsequently formed into micron-sized urchin-like Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs with the BET surfaces area ≈20.99&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202412449-fig-0002">2d</a>). The characteristic of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs was further characterized with the HRTEM and high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscope (HAADF-STEM), as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Clearly, the well-defined lattice interlayer spacing with 0.275&nbsp;nm was observed, which is larger than the typical 0.243&nbsp;nm spacing associated with the (311) plane of the cubic Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> phase, which can be attributed to Fe lattice doping. The incorporation of Fe increases bond lengths within the crystal lattice, which in turn leads to an expansion in ion (Na<sup>+</sup>) transport channels. Meanwhile, the high-resolution HAADF-STEM image corresponding elemental energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mapping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0001">1d–g</a>) demonstrated that Co, Fe, and O were uniformly dispersed throughout the nanoparticles, suggesting that Fe were introduced during the hydrothermal process. Additionally, we obtained the atomic ratio of Co/Fe around 11:1 by the inductively coupled plasma-optical emission spectrometer (ICP-OES) technique, as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), further confirming the content of iron element. As a result, Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs have been successfully obtained by the co-precipitation doping synthesis method.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202412449-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/513e973e-9ccd-4baa-84bd-ae99c9a235b8/smll202412449-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/513e973e-9ccd-4baa-84bd-ae99c9a235b8/smll202412449-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1454eac4-e2bb-4859-9235-a39837cb52bf/smll202412449-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/513e973e-9ccd-4baa-84bd-ae99c9a235b8/smll202412449-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202412449-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202412449" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

SEM images of b) Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs (inset in b is a SEM image of nanoparticles with high magnification, scale bar is 200&nbsp;nm), c) Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs (inset in b is a SEM image of nanowires with high magnification, scale bar is 20&nbsp;nm). d) HAADF-STEM image and elemental mapping analysis of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs; e) Co, f) Fe, and g) O elements, scale bar is 0.5&nbsp;µm.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202412449-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0d79181d-3799-4f52-a0df-03ba47fb194c/smll202412449-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0d79181d-3799-4f52-a0df-03ba47fb194c/smll202412449-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/87f774a1-00b1-4943-a7e3-5c90bf2a92b5/smll202412449-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0d79181d-3799-4f52-a0df-03ba47fb194c/smll202412449-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202412449-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202412449" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structure characterizations of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs sample by spectroscopes. a) XRD pattern; b) FTIR spectrum; c) UV–vis spectrum; d) Nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms; e) XPS survey spectrum and e) Mössbauer spectra of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs at room temperature.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>We also note that the Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles (Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs) samples present the same crystal structure, as evidenced by the corresponding XRD patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The X-ray diffraction (XRD) technique to analyze the crystallographic structure of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs. There are four prominent peaks located at 2θ = 31.2°, 36.8°, 59.3°, and 65.2°, as shown Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0002">2a</a>, which can be indexed to (220), (311), (511), and (440) diffractions of cubic Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> phase (PDF # 43–1003), respectively. The lattice parameters of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs obtained through Rietveld refinement of XRD data using the FullProf Suite (version 5.10) are listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The lattice parameters increase with increasing iron lattice doping, which is consistent with the detailed XRD (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The lattice parameters further indicate that iron lattice doping increases the metal-oxygen bond length. And the average crystallite size of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> calculated using Scherrer's formula is listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The decrease in average crystallite size with increasing doping suggests that doping may reduce average crystallite size and increase specific surface area. This result is consistent with the results of Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area analysis. The values of the lattice distortion are calculated in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) by the Williamson–Hall formula.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0029_R_d3386538e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Phys. Rev. B">29</a>-<a href="#smll202412449-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0031_R_d3386538e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Mater. Chem. Phys.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The lattice distortion values (<b>ε<sub>hkl</sub></b>) of the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs samples are estimated to be 2.39&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−2</sup> and 2.76&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−2</sup>, respectively. It was found that Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs exhibited higher lattice distortion values than that of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> because of iron element incorporation into the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> lattice. As Fe doping increases, the films’ surface roughness decreases, likely due to the change in lattice size shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0032_R_d3386538e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Surf. Topogr. -Metrol. Prop.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This occurs due to iron integrating into the host cobalt oxide lattice, which is known to ensure a significant uniform electrochemical surface reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0033_R_d3386538e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Heliyon">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, iron-induced dopant sites can be used as active sites to adsorb Na<sup>+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0034_R_d3386538e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Processes">34</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0035_R_d3386538e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nat. Commun.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, incorporating iron ions into the cobalt oxide lattice decreases crystallite size and increases lattice parameters. Additionally, the formation of subtle lattice distortions at the iron-doped sites contributes to a more uniform electrochemical surface reaction. The Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectra of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs and Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0002">2b</a>. The peak at roughly 3427&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the O─H stretching vibration of water molecules, while the smaller peak at ≈1648&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> may be caused by the O─H stretching and bending modes of water molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0033_R_d3386538e942" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Heliyon">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intensity of the OH stretching vibration absorption peak is reduced with adding iron ions, to Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs. Additionally, the peak roughly between 1384&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> matches the O─H─Co stretching vibration. Finally, the characteristic peaks at 664 and 574&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are connected to the stretching vibrations of the metal–oxygen (M–O) bond, which supports the spinel structure of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs. Due to the addition of iron, the band shifted slightly toward the higher wavenumber region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0033_R_d3386538e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Heliyon">33</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0036_R_d3386538e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - AIP Conf. Proc.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the FTIR spectra investigation are well matching with XRD data.</p>
            
            <p>The UV–vis spectra (UV–vis) of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs samples are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0002">2c</a>. The absorption spectrum is the presence of two pronounced absorption edges in the visible region in UV–vis spectra, which are ascribed to the ligand-to-metal charge transmission result of (E<sub>g2</sub>, O<sup>2−</sup> → Co<sup>2+</sup>) and (E<sub>g1</sub>, O<sup>2−</sup> → Co<sup>3+</sup>) in Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. The optical bandgap of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs and Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs sample were calculated using Tauc relation from Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0036_R_d3386538e1013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - AIP Conf. Proc.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Tauc plot of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs were calculated by extrapolating the linear part of these plots of (αhν)<sup>2</sup> axis to (hν) axis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0033_R_d3386538e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Heliyon">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The bandgap values are decreased due to the addition Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The maximum bandgap (E<sub>g2</sub>) for Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs is assigned to the valence to conduction band excitation. In contrast, the minimum bandgap (E<sub>g1</sub>) is assigned to the O<sup>2−</sup> → Co<sup>3+</sup> charge transfer at Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs where Co<sup>3+</sup> ions are located below the conduction band; due to this impurity energy levels are created in the bandgap region. In contrast, Fe contributes to hole generation and increases its role with the number of charge carriers (holes) that contribute to the conductivity of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs. DFT calculations will further validate this result (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0005">5c,d</a>). The four-point probe conductivity measurements presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) reveal a clear and systematic trend: the electrical conductivity of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs improves progressively with increasing Fe doping levels. This enhancement in conductivity can be attributed to Fe-doped electronic structure modification, facilitating charge carrier mobility through the lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0037_R_d3386538e1086" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Alloys Compd.">37</a>-<a href="#smll202412449-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0040_R_d3386538e1086" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the 6% Fe-doped sample achieves the highest electrical conductivity among the tested compositions, with a value of 11.64&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−3</sup>&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, underscoring the optimal balance between doping concentration and electrical conduction.</p>
            
            <p>The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption was measured at 77&nbsp;K with a relative pressure P/P<sub>0</sub> ranging from 0.029 to 0.99. From these adsorption/desorption curves, BET-specific surface areas and corresponding Barrett-Joyner-Halenda (BJH) pore sizes were calculated for Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0002">2d</a>. These curves show the most significant number of pores distribution ≈5–15&nbsp;nm for all Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs. This pore size distribution range could help boost the diffusion kinetics inside the electrode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0034_R_d3386538e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Processes">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) lists measured BJH pore radius, and BET surface area of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs samples. The reported higher surface area, in the range of 5.70–20.99&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, of samples indicates that the hydrated electrolyte ions could have a high contact area at the electrolyte/electrode surface for the Faradaic redox reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0011_R_d3386538e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The enhancement in electrochemistry activity may have been influenced by the expansion of active sites resulting from the increased surface area, as well as the improved conductivity due to the reduction in the bandgap.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0034_R_d3386538e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Processes">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The electron interactions and the surface composition of the elements of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs were also revealed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0002">2e</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0002">2e</a>, the full XPS survey of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs exhibits the existence of cobalt, iron, and oxygen elements. The contents of Co and Fe were 20.60% and 3.36%, respectively, calculated from the XPS data. Especially, there are Fe 2p signal peaks were observed in the full XPS survey of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs. The Co 2p spectrum of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs can be deconvoluted into four distinct peaks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), with the peaks at 779.7 and 794.8&nbsp;eV corresponding to the Co<sup>3+</sup> components of the 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> states, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0041_R_d3386538e1194" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - IOP Conf. Ser.: Mater. Sci. Eng.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the peaks at 781.5 and 796.9&nbsp;eV represent the binding energies of Co<sup>2+</sup> for the 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> states. Compared to Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs, the Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks in the XPS spectrum of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs shift toward higher binding energies, indicating changes in the electronic environment due to iron lattice doping. Notably, both Co<sup>3+</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup> signals are present in Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, as shown in the XPS fine spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), along with clear satellite peaks. The observed decrease in the mole ratio of Co<sup>3+</sup> relative to Co<sup>2+</sup> confirms that Fe<sup>3</sup>⁺ substitution for Co<sup>3+</sup> occurs at octahedral sites. This substitution reduces the proportion of Co<sup>3+</sup>, increasing the presence of Co<sup>2+</sup>. The core-level O 2p spectrum of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the peaks located at 528.2, 530, and 532.1&nbsp;eV can be ascribed to the metal-oxide bonds. Among these the peak at 530.0&nbsp;eV should be attributed to the lattice oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0042_R_d3386538e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nanoscale">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 532.1&nbsp;eV are the surface oxygen and water molecules adsorbed on the surface element.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0021_R_d3386538e1263" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Sci.">21</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0043_R_d3386538e1263" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high peak area content at 532.1&nbsp;eV also confirmed to the rich porous structure of the Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs. Compared with those of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs, the O 1s in the XPS spectrum of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs positively shift ≈2.24&nbsp;eV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The replacement of Co<sup>3+</sup> by Fe<sup>3+</sup> is further supported by the shift in binding energy observed for the O 1s peak (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Due to the slightly lower electronegativity of Fe<sup>3+</sup> (1.83) compared to Co<sup>3+</sup> (1.88), the electron density around oxygen atoms decreases, leading to an increase in the binding energy of the O 1s peak, which consequently shifts to higher binding energies. Therefore, the above results confirm Fe in the Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs, and the Fe<sup>3+</sup> replaces part of Co<sup>3+</sup> at octahedral sites in the Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs. We further analyze the XPS spectra of Fe 2p as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), the Fe 2p doublet peaks at 710.0, 711,8, and 713.9&nbsp;eV are correlated with the Fe<sup>3+</sup>, respectively, which can be attributed to the metal-oxide bonds of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0044_R_d3386538e1330" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">44</a>-<a href="#smll202412449-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0046_R_d3386538e1330" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Top. Catal.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Mössbauer spectrum of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs reveals again that the doped iron exists exclusively in the Fe(III) oxidation state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412449-fig-0002">2f</a>). This result is consistent with the XPS data (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Both primary peaks, represented by the dark and light green lines, indicate Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions in octahedral coordination. The dark green line is associated with Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions having a higher hyperfine magnetic field (37.578 T), which is characteristic of the spinel structure in Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0047_R_d3386538e1357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Mater. Res.">47</a>, <a href="#smll202412449-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0048_R_d3386538e1357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the light green line corresponds to Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions with a slightly lower field (33.947 T). This may be due to the difference in the local environment due to lattice distortion caused by iron lattice doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412449-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412449-bib-0049_R_d3386538e1364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nat. Commun.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), the isomer shift (δ) of <sup>57</sup>Fe at the octahedral site increases from 0.35 to 0.37 mm<sup>&nbsp;</sup>s<sup>−1</sup>. An increase in δ might reflect a shift in the local chemical environment due to bonding length changes.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202412449-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202412449-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The electrochemical performance of the Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs electrode was evaluated by assembling half-cell SIBs. As displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202412449-fig-0003">3a</a>, the initial five cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs electrode at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> within the potential window of 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V to reveal the sodiation/desodiation reaction mechanism. The shapes of the CV curves and current intensity are well maintained after the first cycle, demonstrating the excellent reversibility for sodiation/desodiation of the Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs electrode in both voltage ranges. Based on the electrochemical performance and ex situ/in situ characterization analysis, the conversion reaction mechanism of Fe<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3-x</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs with Na metal may occur in two steps, which can be expressed as following equations:

               <figure class="figure" id="smll202412449-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6ad27524-a0ef-45bd-b2f2-786417998b2b/smll202412449-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
